Conditions for 'Ushr on Crops According to Sahibayn
Explains which crops are subject to 'ushr according to Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Sahibayn's condition of 'permanent fruit' to determine whether a given crop is subject to 'ushr.

Core Idea
The Hanafi 'ushr is due only on crops that last a year as staple food or general benefit.

The Hanafi school, through Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), establishes a clear condition for the obligation of 'ushr on agricultural produce: the crop must have a 'permanent fruit' (thamara baqiya) that remains viable for a year without artificial preservation, and it must be either a staple food or provide a general benefit. This lesson unpacks that condition and applies it to the extensive list of crops mentioned in the text, so you can confidently classify produce for zakat purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
Locate the statement by the Sahibayn: 'Ushr is not obligatory except on that which has a permanent fruit.' Understand that 'permanent fruit' means the crop's substance remains intact for one year without effort (like drying) and it is either a staple food (qutat) or used for widespread benefit (intifa' 'am).
The term 'permanent fruit' is a technical condition, not literal botanical fruit.
2
Classify crops into the two categories
From the commentary, separate crops into those that are staple foods (e.g., wheat, barley, rice, lentils, dates) and those that provide general benefit but are not staple foods (e.g., saffron, pepper, cumin, sesame oil). Note that medicines and purely seasonal vegetables are excluded.
General benefit means the item is widely used as a condiment or in trade, not just personal consumption.
3
Apply the rule to ambiguous crops
Use the examples given: Persian sugarcane is exempt unless deliberately cultivated as a plantation; sesame and olives: if pressed before 'ushr is taken, the 'ushr is on the oil, not the plant; walnuts, almonds, onions, garlic are subject (sound opinion); seeds of vegetables are exempt because they are only for planting, not eating.
The ruling depends on the primary use of the crop (edible/beneficial vs. agricultural production only).
4
Note the exceptions
Identify crops that are clearly exempt: medicines (thyme, nigella, fenugreek), industrial plants (marshmallow, woad, saltwort), tree exudates (tar, gum), and vegetable seeds (eggplant, carrot, cucumber). These do not meet the 'permanent fruit' condition because they lack storability or general nutritional benefit.
The exception for Persian sugarcane on the edges of land shows location and cultivation method can affect the ruling.

وأما القصب الفارسي: فلا شيء فيه؛ لأنه لا يستنبت.
وهذا إذا كان في أطراف الأرض، أما إذا اتخذ أرضه مَقصَبَةً، أو مشجرة، أو منبتاً للحشيش، أو ساق إليه الماء، ومنع الناس منه: يجب فيه العشر.
قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا يجب العشر إلا فيما له ثمرةً باقية).
أي تبقى عينه حولاً من غير تكلُّف، ولا تشميس، مما يقتات، كالحنطة والشعير والذرة والدخن والأرز والجَاوَرْس (۱) والعدس والماش واللوبيا، والحمص والبرعي والهندباء والتمر والزبيب، وما أشبه ذلك مما يُقصد به الأكل، وهو يبقى سنةً، أو يُنتفع به انتفاعاً عاماً، كالزعفران والعصفر والفلفل والكمون والخردل والكزبرة: ففيه العشر.

وكذا الزيتون على هذا.
ويجب العشر في الجوز واللوز والبصل والثوم، في الصحيح.
ولا عُشر في الأدوية، كالسعتر والشونيز والحلف والحلبة.
(۱) حبُّ يُشبه الذُرة، وهو أصغر منه. المصباح المنير (جرس).
ولا شيء في الخطمي والوسمة (۱) وبزره، ولا في الأشنان، ولا فيما يخرج من الخشب كالقطران والسلب والقت والصمغ.
ولا شيء في بزر الباذنجان والجزر ولا في بزر القثاء والبطيخ والدباء والخيار ؛ لأن هذه الأشياء لا تصلح إلا للزراعة، دون الأكل.
Chapter on Zakat of Crops and Fruits.
They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: 'Ushr is not obligatory except for that which has a permanent fruit.
As for Persian sugarcane: there is nothing on it because it is not planted perennially.
This is when it is on the edges of the land. But if he makes his land a sugarcane plantation, or a tree plantation, or a place where grass grows, or he brings water to it and prevents people from it, then 'ushr is obligatory.
His statement: (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: 'Ushr is not obligatory except for that which has a permanent fruit).
Meaning, its substance remains for a year without effort or sun-drying, from what is a staple food, like wheat, barley, corn, millet, rice, jawars (a type of grain), lentils, mung beans, cowpeas, chickpeas, barley (?) and chicory, dates, raisins, and the like of what is intended for eating, and it lasts for a year, or it is used in a general benefit, like saffron, safflower, pepper, cumin, mustard, coriander: on these is 'ushr.
On sesame: 'ushr. If it is pressed before the 'ushr is taken from it, it is taken from its oil, and nothing is taken from the plant.
Likewise, olives on the same basis.
'Ushr is obligatory on walnuts, almonds, onions, and garlic, according to the correct view.
There is no 'ushr on medicines, like thyme, nigella seed, hay leek, and fenugreek.
(1) A grain resembling corn, but smaller. Al-Misbah al-Munir (jaras).
There is nothing on marshmallow, woad (a plant used for dye) and its seeds, nor on saltwort, nor on what comes out of wood, like tar, sap, resin, and gum.
There is nothing on seeds of eggplant, carrot, cucumber, melon, pumpkin, and cucumber; because these things are only suitable for cultivation, not for eating.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on zakat of crops and fruits. It first presents the position of Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) that the 'ushr (tithe) is obligatory only on crops that have a permanent fruit (thamar baqiyah). 'Permanent fruit' means the produce remains in its original state for a year without artificial preservation, and it is either a staple food (qut) that sustains people, or it yields a general widespread benefit (intifa' 'am) such as spices and dye plants. The text then explains that Persian sugarcane (qasab farisi) is exempt because it is not a perennial crop; but if one deliberately cultivates his land as a plantation of sugarcane, trees, or grass, and irrigates it while preventing public access, then 'ushr becomes due. The commentary clarifies the Sahibayn's definition: the fruit must remain for a year without effort or sun-drying, and lists examples of staple grains (wheat, barley, corn, etc.) and spices (saffron, pepper, etc.) on which 'ushr is due. For sesame and olives, if pressed before taking 'ushr, the 'ushr is taken from the oil, not the plant. Walnuts, almonds, onions, and garlic are included according to the sound position. Medicines and herbs like thyme, nigella, and fenugreek are exempt. Also exempt are industrial plants like marshmallow, woad, saltwort, and tree exudates (tar, gum). Vegetable seeds like eggplant, carrot, cucumber, etc. are exempt because they are only for planting, not eating. The ruling emphasizes the condition that the crop must be intended for consumption or general benefit, not merely for cultivation.

Key Terms
العشر (al-'ushr): The tithe of one-tenth (or one-twentieth for irrigated land) due as zakat on agricultural produce.
ثمرة باقية (thamara baqiya): Permanent fruit that remains in its original state for a year without effort or sun-drying.
قتات (qutat): Staple food that sustains people, such as grains and legumes.

C) It is not a staple food
D) It is considered a medicine

Key Takeaway
The obligation of 'ushr in the Hanafi school depends on the crop having a permanent fruit that either serves as a staple food or provides general benefit, as defined by the Sahibayn.

Nisab of 'Ushr: Five Awsuk
Defines the nisab and measurement units for 'ushr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum nisab for 'ushr and define a wasq in terms of sa' and alternative measures like mann.

Core Idea
Zakat on crops is obligatory only when produce reaches five awsuq – approximately 300 sa' or the load of a camel.

The obligation of 'ushr (zakat on crops) depends on a specific minimum threshold. This passage from al-Quduri's matn establishes that threshold as five awsuq. Understanding the exact measure is critical for correct application, as it distinguishes the liable from the exempt. The commentary then unpacks the definition of a wasq using the Prophet's sa' and alternative scholarly measurements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the core legal ruling: 'Ushr is wajib (obligatory) when the produce reaches five awsuq. This is a condition (shart) for the obligation.
The nisab is the minimum threshold; below it, no zakat is due.
2
Define the wasq
The matn defines a wasq as 60 sa' using the Prophet's (s) sa'. Internalize this conversion: 1 wasq = 60 sa'.
The sa' is a volumetric unit, not weight-based.
3
Examine alternative measures
The commentary gives alternative expressions: 1 wasq = 240 mann (based on al-Sihah). Also, al-Sayrafi gives a calculation using the Sanqari mudd: 1 wasq = 24 mann (this appears contradictory but likely means each wasq is 24 mann, so 5 awsuq = 120 mann).
The precise measurement varies, but the legal nisab remains five awsuq.
4
Confirm unanimity
Note that the nisab of five awsuq is agreed upon (muttafaq 'alayh) among the schools. Disagreements exist only on the exact volume of a sa', not on the number of awsuq.
Cross-madhhab agreement strengthens the ruling.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a farmer harvests 250 sa' of wheat, this is less than 300 sa' (5 x 60), so no 'ushr is due. If he harvests 350 sa', the nisab is met and 'ushr must be paid.
The nisab is the same for all types of grains and fruits subject to 'ushr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الزروع والثمار
إذا بلغت خمسة أَوْسُقٍ.
والوَسقُ : ستون صاعاً بصاع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم .
قوله: (إذا بلغ خمسة أوسق، والوسق: ستون صاعاً بصاع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم).
قال في «الصحاح»: الوسق بكسر الواو ، والوسق: مائتان وأربعون مَنَّا ، وهو عبارة عن حمل جَمَل ، وجملة الأوساق الخمسة: ثلاثمائة صاع.
وقال الصيرفي رحمه الله : الصاع: أربعة أمداد بالمد السنقري فيكون الوسق: أربعة وعشرين منَّا، فالخمسة الأوسق على هذا: أربعة وعشرين منَّا.
وعلى تخريج أن الصاع خمسة أرطال وثلث مدان ونصف بالسنقري؛ لأن نسبة خمسة أرطال وثلث من ثمانية أرطال : ثلثاها، فخُذ ثلثي أربعة أمداد الأربع: تجده مدين ونصفاً.
وقيل : يجب في الشونيز العشر، وهو حبة السوداء.
Chapter on Zakat of Crops and Fruits.
When it reaches five awsuq.
And the wasq is sixty sa' according to the sa' of the Prophet (peace be upon him).
His statement: (When it reaches five awsuq, and the wasq is sixty sa' according to the sa' of the Prophet (peace be upon him)).
He said in 'al-Sihah': The wasq (with kasr of the waw) is two hundred and forty mann, and it is the load of a camel. The total of five awsuq is three hundred sa'.
And al-Sayrafi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The sa' is four mudd according to the mudd of al-Sanqari, so the wasq is twenty-four mann. Thus five awsuq on this basis are twenty-four mann.
And on the derivation that the sa' is five ratl and one-third, and two mudd and a half according to al-Sanqari; because the ratio of five and one-third ratl to eight ratl is two-thirds. So take two-thirds of four mudd of the four: you will find it is two mudd and a half.
And it is said: 'Ushr is due on shuniz (nigella seed), which is the black seed.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This continuation of the chapter establishes the minimum threshold (nisab) for 'ushr to be obligatory. The matn states that 'ushr is due when the produce reaches five awsuq. The commentary explains the definition of wasq: it is sixty sa' according to the sa' of the Prophet (sallallahu 'alayhi wa sallam). Al-Sihah dictionary states that a wasq is 240 mann, which is the load of a camel, and five awsuq equal 300 sa'. The commentator then includes al-Sayrafi's calculation, noting that the sa' is 4 mudd using the sanqari measurement (a specific standard), making the wasq 24 mann, so five awsuq would be 120 mann? But the text says 'twenty-four mann' which might be a typo? Actually, it says 'fakhamsatu al-usuq 'ala hadha: arba'ata wa 'ishrina mann' — that seems contradictory. It might mean each wasq is 24 mann, so five awsuq would be 120 mann. The text then gives an alternative derivation: the sa' is 5½ ratl and 2½ mudd (sanqari), and explains the proportion. This detailed measurement ensures precision in determining the nisab. The passage ends with a note that according to some, 'ushr is due on shuniz (nigella seed), which is the black seed. This lesson emphasizes the quantitative condition for 'ushr obligation.

Key Terms
وسق (wasq): A measure equal to 60 sa' or 240 mann, approximately the load of a camel.
صاع (sa'): A unit of volume equal to 4 mudd, approximately 2.5 kg of wheat according to Hanafi standard.
من (mann): A unit of weight, approximately 0.75 sa' (according to Hanafi conversion).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The nisab for 'ushr is five awsuq (each of 60 Prophet's sa'), a condition universally agreed upon, and understanding this threshold is fundamental to correct zakat payment.

Nisab for Zakat on Grains and Fruits: Five Awsuq
Defines the minimum threshold for zakat on crops

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab for zakat on grains and fruits and identify which crops are exempt from zakat according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Zakat on crops is due only when the produce reaches five awsuq (300 sa'), not on vegetables or wood extracts.

This lesson establishes the minimum threshold (nisab) for zakat on agricultural produce. The Hanafi school defines this threshold as five awsuq, with each wasq being sixty sa' by the Prophet's sa'. The passage also clarifies which crops are subject to zakat and which are exempt, based on their edibility and staple nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab
Locate the ruling that zakat becomes obligatory when the produce reaches five awsuq. Note that this is a threshold, not a fixed amount for all crops.
2
Define the wasq and sa'
Understand that the wasq is defined as 60 sa' using the Prophet's sa'. The sa' itself is a volumetric measure equivalent to 4 amdad. This customary measurement is authoritative for the Hanafi school.
3
Distinguish zakatable from exempt items
Review the list: shuniz (black seed) is subject to zakat, while khatmi, wasma, ushnan, wood extracts (qatran, salab, qatt, samgh), and seeds of vegetables (eggplant, carrot, cucumber, etc.) are exempt because they are only for planting, not for eating.
Exempt items are those not considered staple grains or fruits.
4
Relate to the hadith proof
The ruling is based on the hadith 'There is no sadaqah on less than five awsuq' (Bukhari and Muslim). This hadith is the primary dalil for the nisab.

Zakat on Vegetables: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'ushr for vegetables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for vegetables, identify the differing positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the mufta bihi view in practice.

Core Idea
Vegetables are exempt from 'ushr unless grown for trade—this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

This passage from the commentary on al-Quduri clarifies a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether vegetables are subject to 'ushr. The matn states the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that no 'ushr is due, while Abu Hanifa is reported to hold a general obligation. The commentary also provides exceptions for trade, onions, and grapes, illustrating the nuanced criteria for zakat on produce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Note that the matn says 'according to the two' (عندهما), indicating that this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, while Imam Abu Hanifa is reported to differ. The issue is whether vegetables (خضروات) are subject to 'ushr (zakat on produce).
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
2
Understand the basis for exclusion
The Sahibayn's position is based on the perishable nature of vegetables—they are not stored like grains and fruits. The definition given: 'what does not have a lasting fruit' (ما ليس له ثمرة باقية). This includes leafy greens (بقول) and soft fruits (رطاب).
Abu Hanifa's general view relies on the broad command to give zakat on all produce.
3
Apply the exception for trade
If vegetables are grown for trade (التجارة), then zakat of trade (زكاة التجارة) is due when the value reaches 200 dirhams. This is agreed upon by all. So the exemption only applies to vegetables intended for personal consumption or not as trade stock.
Trade zakat is different from 'ushr; it is based on value.
4
Analyze the specific case of onions
A riwaya from Muhammad states that onions do have 'ushr because they are stored, widely used, and measurable (يدخل تحت الكيل). This shows that the key factor is durability and being a staple, not just being a vegetable.
Onions are an exception within the exception.
5
Apply the rule to grapes
Grapes are subject to 'ushr if the dried raisins reach five awsuq. This is because grapes can be stored as raisins. However, if the variety is thin and only produces juice, no 'ushr is due. This reinforces the principle that only staple, measurable produce is subject to 'ushr.
The threshold of five awsuq (approx. 653 kg) is the nisab for dried fruits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس في الخضروات عندهما عُشر.
* قوله : (وليس في الخضراوات عندهما عُشر).
٢١١ فإن كانت للتجارة تجب فيها زكاة التجارة، بالاتفاق إذا بلغت قيمتها مائتي درهم. والخضراوات : ما ليس له ثمرة باقيةٌ، كالبُقُول، والرطاب. فالبُقُول : كالكراث والبقل والسلق، ونحو ذلك. والرطاب : كالقثّاء والبطيخ والباذنجان والسفرجل والرمان والتفاح، وأشباه ذلك.
وأما البصل : فروى محمد أن فيه العشر ؛ لأنه يبقى في أيدي الناس، وينتفع به انتفاعاً عاماً، ويدخل تحت الكيل.
والعنب إن كان ء منه الزبيب مقدار خمسة أوسق: ففيه العشر، يجيء وذلك بأن يُخرَص جافاً، فإن بلغ مقدار ذلك: وَجَبَ فيه العشر، أو نصفه إن كان يُسقى بغَرْب أو دالية. وإن لم يبلغ ذلك: فلا شيء فيه. وعن محمد : إذا كان العنب رقيقاً لا يصلح إلا للماء، ولا يجيء الزبيب: لا شيء فيه وإن كثر.
And there is no 'ushr on vegetables according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). * His statement: (And there is no 'ushr on vegetables according to the two). 211 If they are for trade, then zakat of trade is obligatory on them, by agreement, when their value reaches two hundred dirhams. And vegetables: what does not have a lasting fruit, like buqul and ratab. So buqul: like leek, greens, chard, and the like. And ratab: like cucumber, watermelon, eggplant, quince, pomegranate, apple, and similar. As for onions: Muhammad narrated that there is 'ushr on them, because they remain in people's hands, are benefited from generally, and come under measurement. And grapes: if from them raisins (amount) of five awsuq, then there is 'ushr on them; it comes by estimating them dry, and if it reaches that amount, 'ushr is obligatory, or half if watered by gharb or daliya. And if it does not reach that, then nothing. And from Muhammad: if grapes are thin, only suitable for water (juice), and raisins do not come, then nothing even if abundant.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage addresses zakat on vegetables. The matn states that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), there is no 'ushr on vegetables. The commentary clarifies that if vegetables are grown for trade, zakat of trade applies when their value reaches the nisab of 200 dirhams, and this is agreed upon. Vegetables are defined as items without lasting fruit, divided into buqul (leafy greens) and ratab (soft fruits). A riwaya from Muhammad states that onions do have 'ushr because they are stored, widely used, and measurable. For grapes, zakat depends on the dried product: if raisins reach five awsuq, 'ushr is due, with half if irrigated artificially. If grapes are thin and only produce juice, no zakat. This shows the nuance in Hanafi fiqh regarding what counts as a staple crop.

Key Terms
الخضروات (al-khudrawat): Vegetables that do not have a lasting fruit (thamar baqiya).
البُقُول (al-buqul): Leafy greens like leek, spinach, chard.
الرطاب (al-ratab): Soft fruits like cucumber, watermelon, eggplant, etc.
العشر (al-'ushr): Zakat due on agricultural produce, typically 1/10 (or 1/20 if irrigated).
زكاة التجارة (zakat al-tijarah): Zakat on merchandise intended for trade, at 2.5% when nisab and hawl are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what is the ruling on zakat for vegetables?
A) 'Ushr is always obligatory
B) No 'ushr, but if for trade, trade zakat applies
C) Half 'ushr is obligatory
D) 'Ushr is obligatory only on onions

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, vegetables are exempt from 'ushr; the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position, with onions as an exception.

Half 'Ushr for Artificially Irrigated Crops
Ruling statement on zakat rate for artificially irrigated crops.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi zakat rate for manually irrigated crops and explain the basis for the reduced rate.

Core Idea
Artificial irrigation reduces zakat to half 'ushr because of the effort and cost involved.

This short matn from Al-Jawhara al-Nayra establishes a key principle in Hanafi zakat: crops that require human or animal effort for irrigation are subject to half the standard rate. The ruling reflects the Islamic legal principle of ease and proportionality, acknowledging the additional burden on the farmer. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly calculating zakat on agricultural produce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on crops that are watered by three specific methods: gharb (bucket by hand), daliya (water-wheel), and saniya (animal-powered device). The ruling is that they owe half 'ushr (5%).
The ruling is wajib, i.e., obligatory, not merely recommended.
2
Understand the common element of the methods
All three methods involve manual or animal effort, as opposed to natural irrigation (rain, rivers). The common element is the additional cost and labour.
This common element (illa) is the basis for the reduced rate.
3
Contrast with full 'ushr
Full 'ushr (10%) applies to crops naturally irrigated by rain or flowing rivers without effort. Half 'ushr is a concession for those who exert effort.
The hadith basis: 'In what is watered by the sky (rain) and springs, 'ushr; and in what is watered by irrigation (manual), half 'ushr.'
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school does not make this distinction; they require full 'ushr regardless of irrigation method.
The khilaf stems from different interpretations of the hadith and the concept of qiyas.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Modern artificial irrigation (pumps, sprinklers) is analogous to the classical methods; the same half 'ushr applies. The illa is the presence of effort and cost.
This is a direct application of the Hanafi principle of istihsan and qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما سُقِيَ بغَرب، أو دالية، أو سانية : ففيه نصف العشر
* قوله: (وما سقي بغرب، أو دالية، أو سانية: ففيه نصف العشر).
And what is watered by gharb, or daliya, or saniya: then half 'ushr is on it. * His statement: (And what is watered by gharb, or daliya, or saniya: then half the 'ushr is on it).
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This matn states a key ruling: crops that are irrigated by manual means (gharb: bucket, daliya: water-wheel, saniya: animal-powered irrigation) are subject to half the full 'ushr, i.e., 5% instead of 10%. This reduction reflects the greater effort and cost involved in artificial irrigation compared to rain-fed or naturally irrigated crops. The ruling is derived from a hadith indicating that crops watered by natural sources get full 'ushr, while those requiring effort get half.

Key Terms
الغرب (al-gharb): A bucket used for drawing water; artificial irrigation by hand.
الدالية (al-daliya): A water-wheel or similar device for irrigation.
السانية (al-saniya): Animal-powered irrigation device.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Half 'ushr (5%)
C) No zakat
D) One-third 'ushr

Key Takeaway
Artificial irrigation triggers half 'ushr (5%) in Hanafi zakat, a reduction from the full 10% for naturally watered crops.

Vegetables Exempt from 'Ushr (Sahibayn's Position)
Ruling statement with expansion on vegetables and trade zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that vegetables are not subject to 'ushr according to the Sahibayn, identify the condition that triggers trade zakat, and recognize the exception for onions.

Core Idea
Vegetables have no 'ushr, but trade zakat applies if intended for commerce; onions are an exception.

This lesson addresses the Hanafi ruling on vegetables and zakat. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, vegetables grown as crops are exempt from the 'ushr (tenth) that applies to certain agricultural produce. However, if the vegetables are grown for trade, they may become subject to zakāt al-tijārah (trade zakat) when their value reaches the niṣāb. The passage also notes a unique narration regarding onions, which are treated differently because of their lasting nature and general utility. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying zakat obligations in agricultural contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The matn states that there is no 'ushr on vegetables according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Understand that this is the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah).
2
Recognize the exception for trade
If vegetables are intended for trade, then trade zakat applies if the value reaches 200 dirhams. This is agreed upon.
3
Define vegetables in Hanafi law
Vegetables are defined as plants without lasting fruit, such as leafy greens (buqūl) and fresh produce (riṭāb). Examples are given.
4
Note the special case of onions
According to a narration from Muhammad, onions incur 'ushr because they are lasting, commonly beneficial, and measurable (under kayl). This is an exception to the general rule.
5
Synthesize the rulings
Combine the above: no 'ushr on vegetables generally, but trade zakat possible; onion may have 'ushr per one narration (but not necessarily the mufta bihi).

Source Text

باب زكاة الزروع والثمار
وليس في الخضروات عندهما عُشر.
قوله: (وليس في الخضراوات عندهما عُشر).
فإن كانت للتجارة تجب فيها زكاة التجارة، بالاتفاق إذا بلغت قيمتها مائتي درهم. والخضراوات : ما ليس له ثمرة باقيةٌ، كالبُقُول، والرطاب.
فالبُقُول : كالكراث والبقل والسلق، ونحو ذلك.
والرطاب : كالقثّاء والبطيخ والباذنجان والسفرجل والرمان والتفاح، وأشباه ذلك.
وأما البصل : فروى محمد أن فيه العشر ؛ لأنه يبقى في أيدي الناس، وينتفع به انتفاعاً عاماً، ويدخل تحت الكيل.
والعنب إن كان ء منه الزبيب مقدار خمسة أوسق: ففيه العشر، يجيء وذلك بأن يُخرَص جافاً، فإن بلغ مقدار ذلك: وَجَبَ فيه العشر، أو نصفه إن كان يُسقى بغَرْب أو دالية.
وإن لم يبلغ ذلك: فلا شيء فيه.
وعن محمد : إذا كان العنب رقيقاً لا يصلح إلا للماء، ولا يجيء الزبيب: لا شيء فيه وإن كثر.
Chapter of Zakat on Crops and Fruits. And there is no tenth on vegetables according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). His saying: (And there is no tenth on vegetables according to the two). If they are for trade, the zakat of trade is obligatory on them, by agreement, if their value reaches two hundred dirhams. And vegetables: that which has no lasting fruit, like leafy greens and fresh produce. Leafy greens: like leek, beet, chard, and the like. Fresh produce: like cucumber, melon, eggplant, quince, pomegranate, apple, and similar. As for onion: Muhammad narrated that there is a tenth on it because it remains in people's hands, they benefit from it generally, and it falls under measurement. And grapes if from them raisins amount to five wasqs: then there is a tenth on them, it comes about by estimating it dry, then if it reaches that amount: the tenth becomes obligatory, or half if it is irrigated by bucket or waterwheel. And if it does not reach that: nothing on it. And from Muhammad: if the grapes are thin, not suitable except for water (making vinegar), and raisins do not come from it: nothing on it even if abundant.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Quduri, explained by the commentator. It states that vegetables do not incur the 'ushr (tenth) according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The commentator expands by noting that if vegetables are grown for trade, then trade zakat applies if their value reaches the niṣāb (200 dirhams), and this is agreed upon. Vegetables are defined as plants that do not have lasting fruit, such as leafy greens (buqūl) like leek and chard, and fresh produce (riṭāb) like cucumber and melon. An exception is made for onions: according to a narration from Muhammad, onions incur 'ushr because they are lasting, commonly beneficial, and measurable. Grapes are then discussed separately: if they yield raisins reaching five wasqs (niṣāb), then 'ushr is due; if artificially irrigated, half 'ushr. If grapes do not reach the niṣāb, no zakah. A narration from Muhammad states that thin grapes used only for vinegar also have no 'ushr even if abundant. The commentary follows the order of the matn, first clarifying the ruling on vegetables, then addressing exceptions and related produce.

Key Terms
خضروات (khadrāwāt): Vegetables: plants without lasting fruit, like leafy greens and fresh produce.
عندهما (indahumā): According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
زكاة التجارة (zakāt al-tijārah): Zakat on trade goods, applicable when vegetables are bought for resale and value reaches niṣāb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When they are grown in a field larger than 5 wasqs
B) When they are intended for trade and their value reaches 200 dirhams
C) When they are irrigated by rainwater
D) When they are harvested before ripening

Key Takeaway
Vegetables are exempt from 'ushr according to the Sahibayn, but trade zakat applies if they are for trade and reach niṣāb; onions have a special narration that may impose 'ushr.

Grapes and the Obligation of 'Ushr
Expansion on grapes and 'ushr with irrigation and quality distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the niṣāb for grapes based on dry raisins, apply the irrigation rule for half 'ushr, and identify when grapes are exempt due to poor quality.

Core Idea
Grapes are subject to 'ushr only if they yield raisins reaching five wasqs, with half 'ushr for artificial irrigation; thin grapes for vinegar are exempt.

This lesson continues the discussion of 'ushr on crops, focusing on grapes. Unlike vegetables, grapes can be subject to the tenth (or half) when the dried raisins reach the niṣāb of five wasqs. The passage also distinguishes between different irrigation methods—natural versus artificial—which affects the rate. Additionally, a narration from Muhammad exempts thin grapes that are only suitable for vinegar, even if abundant. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying agricultural zakat to vineyards.

Guided Path
1
Identify the niṣāb for grapes
Grapes are subject to 'ushr only if the dried raisins from the harvest amount to five wasqs. The estimation is made on the dry produce, not the fresh fruit.
2
Understand the irrigation rule
If the vineyard is irrigated naturally (e.g., rainwater), the full tenth (10%) is due. If irrigated artificially (e.g., by bucket or waterwheel), half of that (5%) is due.
3
Recognize when no 'ushr is due
If the yield does not reach the niṣāb of five wasqs of raisins, no 'ushr is payable. Also, if the grapes are of poor quality, thin, and only usable for making vinegar (not raisins), there is no 'ushr even if abundant.
4
Note the exception for thin grapes
According to a narration from Muhammad, if the grapes are so thin that they cannot produce raisins and are only good for vinegar (or water), no 'ushr is due, regardless of quantity.
5
Synthesize the application
To determine 'ushr on grapes: measure the dry raisin yield; if ≥5 wasqs, apply 10% or 5% depending on irrigation; if yield is below or grapes are thin, no 'ushr.

Half 'Ushr for Artificially Irrigated Crops: The Basic Ruling
Defines irrigation terms and prescribes half ushr for non-natural watering

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that crops irrigated by artificial means are zakatable at half the ushr rate (5%) and identify the three specific irrigation tools mentioned in the Hanafi texts.

Core Idea
Artificially irrigated crops are zakatable at 5% (half of 10%) because of the added expense of irrigation.

This lesson presents the foundational Hanafi ruling on zakah for crops that are not naturally watered. The matn specifies that if watering is done by a leather bucket (gharb), a waterwheel (dāliyah), or a camel-driven bucket (sāniyah), then only half the ushr is due. The commentator defines these terms to clarify what constitutes artificial irrigation. Understanding this ruling is essential because it establishes a reduced rate that reflects the higher cost and effort of artificial watering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its rate
The matn states: 'What is irrigated by gharb, dāliyah, or sāniyah: half the ushr is due.' This means the zakah rate is 5% instead of 10%.
2
Define the irrigation tools
The commentator explains: dāliyah is a waterwheel, sāniyah is a camel used for drawing water. Gharb is a leather bucket. These represent artificial, effort-intensive methods.
3
Understand the legal basis
The reduced rate is due to the additional expense (mu'na) of artificial irrigation, a principle accepted by the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (وما سقي بغَرب، أو دالية، أو سانية: ففيه نصف العشر)
الدالية : الدولاب
والسانية : البعير الذي يستقى به الماء.
* قوله : (على القولين أي على اختلاف القولين: عند أبي حنيفة: لا يشترط النصاب والبقاء، وعندهما يشترط.
ولو سقي الزرع في بعض السنة سيحاً، وفي بعضها بالغرب: فالمعتبر الأغلب من ذلك، كما في السوائم إذا علفها صاحبها في الحول.
His saying: (And what is irrigated by a leather bucket, or a waterwheel, or a camel drawing water: then half the tenth is due). The dāliyah: the waterwheel. The sāniyah: the camel that draws water with it. His saying: (On the two sayings – i.e., on the difference of the two sayings: according to Abu Hanifa, niṣāb and continuity are not required, and according to the two they are required. And if the crop is irrigated in part of the year by flowing water (saiḥ) and in part by bucket, then the majority is considered, just as with grazing livestock if the owner feeds them during the year.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage contains a matn ruling and its commentary. The matn states that crops irrigated by artificial means such as a leather bucket (gharb), a waterwheel (dāliyah), or a camel-driven bucket (sāniyah) are subject to half the 'ushr rate (5% instead of 10%). The commentator defines the terms: dāliyah is a waterwheel, sāniyah is a camel used to draw water. Then the commentator notes a difference of opinion: according to Abu Hanifa, the conditions of niṣāb (minimum quantity) and continuity (of irrigation method) are not required; according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they are required. Finally, the commentator gives a rule for mixed irrigation: if some months the crop is irrigated naturally (saiḥ) and other months artificially, the majority method determines the rate. This is analogous to the rule for grazing livestock: if the owner feeds them for most of the year, the zakah rule changes accordingly.

Key Terms
غَرْب (gharb): A leather bucket used to draw water for irrigation.
دالية (dāliyah): A waterwheel for lifting water.
سانية (sāniyah): A camel used to draw water from a well.
نصف العشر (niṣf al-ʿushr): Half of the tenth, i.e., 5% of the crop.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a crop irrigated by flowing water (saiḥ) for 4 months and by bucket for 8 months?
A) Full 'ushr because saiḥ is natural
B) Half 'ushr because the bucket (artificial) is the majority
C) No 'ushr because it is mixed
D) Both half and full 'ushr apply proportionally

Key Takeaway
Artificially irrigated crops are zakatable at half the ushr rate (5%) due to the additional cost of irrigation.

Intra-Hanafi Differences on Nisab and Mixed Irrigation for Half 'Ushr
Defines irrigation terms and prescribes half ushr for non-natural watering

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the conditions of nisab and continuity for half ushr, and apply the rule for determining the zakah rate when irrigation is mixed between natural and artificial.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa does not require nisab for half ushr; the Sahibayn do; and when irrigation is mixed, the majority method determines the rate.

This lesson delves into the commentary that follows the matn ruling. The commentator notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that half ushr is due regardless of the quantity (nisab) or the continuity of artificial irrigation, while his two disciples require both nisab and a majority of artificial watering. Additionally, a practical rule for mixed irrigation is given, supported by an analogy to grazing livestock. Grasping these nuances is crucial for correct fatwa in complex agricultural scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ikhtilaf
According to Abu Hanifa, half ushr is due on any artificially irrigated crop, even if small quantity or only partially watered by bucket. The Sahibayn require that the crop reaches nisab (5 wasq) and that the majority of irrigation be artificial.
2
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
If a crop is below nisab, Abu Hanifa still requires half ushr, but the Sahibayn exempt it. Similarly, if artificial irrigation is less than half, Abu Hanifa still applies half ushr, while the Sahibayn would apply full ushr (since natural is majority).
3
Apply the mixed irrigation rule
The commentator states: If the crop is irrigated in part by flowing water (saih) and in part by bucket, then the majority (al-ghalib) is considered. This determines whether the rate is full ushr (if saih majority) or half ushr (if artificial majority).
4
Recognize the analogy to livestock
The same principle applies to saima (grazing livestock): if the owner feeds them artificially for most of the year, the zakah rule changes accordingly.

ولو سقي الزرع في بعض السنة سيحاً، وفي بعضها بالغرب: فالمعتبر الأغلب من ذلك، كما في السوائم إذا علفها صاحبها في الحول.

This passage contains a matn ruling and its commentary. Timing of 'Ushr Obligation: Three Hanafi Positions
Presents intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on timing of 'ushr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the timing of 'ushr obligation and apply the practical consequence (thamarah) to determine liability for consumed produce.

Core Idea
The timing of 'ushr obligation in the Hanafi school ranges from fruit appearance (Abu Hanifa) to threshing floor (Muhammad), affecting liability for what is consumed.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on when exactly 'ushr (the tenth) becomes wajib on agricultural produce. Understanding this dispute is crucial for calculating zakah correctly, especially when some produce is consumed before the obligation is fully settled. The practical consequence (thamarah) directly affects whether the grower must pay 'ushr on what they ate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the disputed legal point? It is the exact time (waqt al-wujūb) when the obligation of 'ushr becomes binding on fruits and crops.
The dispute is about the trigger for zakah, not the rate or conditions.
2
State each imam's position
List the three views: (1) Abu Hanifa and Zufar: at appearance of fruit and safety from spoilage; (2) Abu Yusuf: at harvest readiness; (3) Muhammad: after harvest and when on threshing floor.
Note that Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's positions are later in the season.
3
Analyze scenario 1: eating unripe grain (ḥabb jarrīsh)
If the owner eats grain after it forms but before harvest, under Abu Hanifa and Zufar he must pay 'ushr for what he ate; under Abu Yusuf and Muhammad he does not pay but the eaten amount counts toward the niṣāb.
The difference hinges on whether the obligation already existed at the time of consumption.
4
Analyze scenario 2: eating after harvest is due but before actual harvest
If one eats after the crop is ready for harvest but before cutting, Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar hold him liable for 'ushr on what he ate; Muhammad does not, because in his view obligation only begins after threshing.
Here Abu Yusuf aligns with Abu Hanifa, diverging from Muhammad.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether the owner must compensate 'ushr for personal consumption. Under earlier obligation views, he pays; under later views, he only counts it towards niṣāb and pays on the remainder.
This affects both individuals and zakah administrators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في وقت وجوب العشر في الثمار والزرع :
فقال أبو حنيفة وزفر يجب عند ظهور الثمرة، والأمن عليها من الفساد وإن لم يستحق الحصاد، إذا بلغت حدا ينتفع بها.
وقال أبو يوسف : عند استحقاق الحصاد.
وقال محمد: إذا حصدت، وصارت في الجرين.
وفائدته : فيما إذا أكل منه شيئاً بعد ما صار حباً جَرِيشاً، أو أطعم غيره منه بالمعروف : فإنه يضمن عُشر ما أكل وأطعم عند أبي حنيفة وزفر. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا يضمن، ويُحتسب به في تكميل الأوسق، ولا يُحتسب به في الوجوب، يعني إذا بلغ المأكول مع الباقي خمسة أوسق : وجب العشر في الباقي، لا غير.
وإن أكل منها بعد ما بلغت الحصاد قبل أن تحصد : ضمن عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وزفر، ولم يضمن عند محمد.
And they differed regarding the time of obligation of the tenth on fruits and crops. Abu Hanifa and Zufar said: It is obligatory at the appearance of the fruit and security from spoilage, even if harvest is not yet due, when it reaches a stage where it can be benefited from. Abu Yusuf said: At the time when harvest becomes due. Muhammad said: When it is harvested and reaches the threshing floor. Its benefit (practical import) is in the case where one eats from it after it becomes unripe grain, or feeds others from it in a reasonable manner: then he guarantees the tenth of what he ate and fed according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: He does not guarantee, and it is counted towards completing the wasqs, not counted in the obligation, meaning if the eaten material together with the remainder reaches five wasqs: the tenth is obligatory on the remainder only, not otherwise. And if one eats from it after it reaches harvest time before it is harvested: he guarantees according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar, and does not guarantee according to Muhammad.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the exact moment when 'ushr becomes obligatory on fruits and crops. Three positions are given: (1) Abu Hanifa and Zufar: obligation arises when the fruit appears and is safe from spoilage, even if unripe, provided it is usable. (2) Abu Yusuf: at the time harvest is due (crops are ripe and ready for cutting). (3) Muhammad: after harvest and when it reaches the threshing floor. The practical consequence (thamarah) is illustrated: if someone eats some grain after it forms but before the obligation according to each view, the liability varies. According to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, since the obligation already formed, he must pay 'ushr on what he ate. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he does not pay for what was eaten, but that amount is counted toward the niṣāb to determine if 'ushr is due on the remainder. A second scenario: if one eats after harvest is due but before actual harvest, then according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar, he guarantees (must pay for what he ate), while according to Muhammad, he does not, because in his view obligation only begins after threshing. This detailed ikhtilaf helps determine when zakah becomes due and affects calculations.

Key Terms
وقت الوجوب (waqt al-wujūb): The time at which the obligation of 'ushr becomes binding.
الثمرة (ath-thamarah): Fruit; the produce of trees or vines.
الحصاد (al-ḥiṣād): Harvest; the time when crops are ready to be cut.
الجرين (al-jarīn): Threshing floor; where harvested grains are processed.
ضمِن (ḍamina): He became liable (to compensate the 'ushr for what was consumed).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, when does 'ushr become obligatory on fruits?
A) When the fruit appears and is safe from spoilage
B) When harvest is due
C) When the crop is on the threshing floor
D) When the fruit is fully ripe

Key Takeaway
The timing of 'ushr obligation in the Hanafi school is not monolithic; three distinct positions exist, and the practical consequence mainly affects liability for consumed produce.

Nisab and Haul in Zakat of Crops: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Mixed Irrigation Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nisab and rule for mixed irrigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on nisab and haul for zakat of crops and apply the majority rule for mixed irrigation.

Core Idea
For crops, nisab and haul are required per the Sahibayn; Abu Hanifa says no. Irrigation method determines the rate.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the conditions for zakat on crops. While Abu Hanifa holds that nisab and annual haul are not required, the two companions insist they are. The relied-upon fatwa follows the companions. Additionally, we learn how to calculate the zakat rate when irrigation methods are mixed: the majority method determines whether 10% or 5% is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
The dispute is about whether nisab (minimum quantity) and haul (one year passing) are required for zakat on crops. Abu Hanifa says no; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say yes.
Nisab for crops is 5 awsuq (approx. 900 liters).
2
State each position
Abu Hanifa: no nisab or haul required. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: nisab and haul required.
The difference is over the interpretation of the verse (6:141) and hadith.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The position of the Sahibayn is the operative one for fatwa. Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah prefers their view. It is also the position of most other schools.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.
4
Apply the mixed irrigation rule
If a crop is partly irrigated by natural water (sayh) and partly by artificial means (dāliyah, sāniyah, gharb), the majority determines the rate. If natural > half, then 10% (full 'ushr); if artificial > half, then 5% (half 'ushr).
If equal, according to some, the more favorable to the farmer is taken (5%), but the passage does not mention this detail.
5
Note the analogy with grazing livestock
The text draws an analogy to livestock (sawā'im): if an animal is fed by the owner for part of the year, the majority of feeding method determines the zakat ruling. Similarly, majority irrigation method determines the rate.
This shows the use of qiyas within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الزروع والثمار. الدالية : الدولاب. والسانية : البعير الذي يستقى به الماء. * قوله : (على القولين أي على اختلاف القولين: عند أبي حنيفة: لا يشترط النصاب والبقاء، وعندهما يشترط. ولو سقي الزرع في بعض السنة سيحاً، وفي بعضها بالغرب: فالمعتبر الأغلب من ذلك، كما في السوائم إذا علفها صاحبها في الحول.
Chapter of Zakat of Crops and Fruits. The dāliyah is the waterwheel, and the sāniyah is the camel used for drawing water. His saying: (on the two opinions) meaning on the difference of the two opinions: according to Abū Ḥanīfa: nisāb and [annual] continuity are not conditions, while according to the two companions they are conditions. If the crop is irrigated in part of the year by flowing water and in part by bucket, the majority is considered, just as in grazing livestock when the owner feeds them during the year.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira opens the chapter on zakāt of crops and fruits. It clarifies that the condition of nisāb and haul (annual cycle) is subject to ikhtilāf within the Ḥanafī school: Abū Ḥanīfa does not require nisāb or continuity for zakāt on crops, while Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad require them. The text then gives a ruling on mixed irrigation: if a crop is irrigated partly by natural (sīḥ) and partly by artificial (gharb) methods, the majority determines the rate: if more than half is natural, then 10% ('ushr) is due; otherwise 5%. The analogy with grazing livestock (sawā'im) is used.

Key Terms
دالية (dāliyah): A waterwheel used for irrigation.
سانية (sāniyah): A camel (or animal) used to draw water for irrigation.
سيح (sayḥ): Water that flows naturally, e.g., rainwater or river water, without manual effort.
غرب (gharb): Bucket or skin used for lifting water by manual effort.
وسق (wasq): A unit of volume measure for crops, equivalent to 60 ṣāʿs (approx. 180 liters).

Key Terms from Source Passage

أ) الأغلب من السقيين.
ب) الأقل منهما.
ج) الأكثر قيمة.
د) مجموع السقيتين معاً.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the relied-upon position is that nisab and haul are required for zakat of crops, and the irrigation method's majority determines the rate.

Disagreement on Timing of Obligation of 'Ushr in Fruits and Crops
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of 'ushr obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the timing of 'ushr obligation and apply them to determine liability for early consumption.

Core Idea
When does 'ushr become wajib? At appearance, harvest, or threshing — each triggers a different liability for early consumption.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the precise moment when 'ushr becomes binding on crops and fruits. The difference has practical consequences if the owner consumes or gifts produce before the obligation is fixed. By examining the three views, you will understand how the jurists balanced benefit and risk in defining liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the views of Abu Hanifa/Zufar (at appearance), Abu Yusuf (at harvest ripeness), and Muhammad (at threshing).
2
Locate the key criterion for each
For each imam, determine the precise event that triggers wajib: appearance, harvest readiness, or actual harvest.
3
Work through the scenario of eating jarish
Apply each view to determine liability if the owner eats raw grain before harvest.
Only Abu Hanifa and Zufar impose liability for jarish.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, liability for early consumption; under others, no liability until later.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Find out which position is relied upon in fatwa: Abu Yusuf's view is preferred by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في وقت وجوب العشر في الثمار والزرع : فقال أبو حنيفة وزفر يجب عند ظهور الثمرة، والأمن عليها من الفساد وإن لم يستحق الحصاد، إذا بلغت حدا ينتفع بها. وقال أبو يوسف : عند استحقاق الحصاد. وقال محمد: إذا حصدت، وصارت في الجرين. وفائدته : فيما إذا أكل منه شيئاً بعد ما صار حباً جَرِيشاً، أو أطعم غيره منه بالمعروف : فإنه يضمن عُشر ما أكل وأطعم عند أبي حنيفة وزفر. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا يضمن، ويُحتسب به في تكميل الأوسق، ولا يُحتسب به في الوجوب، يعني إذا بلغ المأكول مع الباقي خمسة أوسق : وجب العشر في الباقي، لا غير. وإن أكل منها بعد ما بلغت الحصاد قبل أن تحصد : ضمن عند ، أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وزفر، ولم يضمن عند محمد. وإن أكل منها بعد ما صارت في الجرين: ضمن، إجماعاً.
They differed regarding the time of obligation of 'ushr in fruits and crops: Abū Ḥanīfa and Zufar said it becomes obligatory when the fruit appears and is safe from spoilage, even if harvest is not yet due, when it reaches a usable stage. Abū Yūsuf said: when harvest becomes due. Muḥammad said: when it is harvested and reaches the threshing floor. The benefit appears in the case where one eats from it after it becomes raw grain (jarīsh) or feeds others without expecting return: he is liable for the 'ushr of what he ate and fed according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Zufar. Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad said: he is not liable, but it is counted toward completing the wasqs, and not counted toward obligation, meaning if what was eaten along with the remainder reaches five wasqs, 'ushr is due on the remainder only, not more. If he ate from it after it reached harvest but before harvesting, he is liable according to Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, and Zufar, but not liable according to Muḥammad. If he ate from it after it was in the threshing floor, he is liable unanimously.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the precise moment when the obligation of 'ushr becomes binding on produce. The difference has practical consequences for liability if the owner consumes or gifts some produce before the obligation becomes fixed. The text gives specific scenarios: eating raw grain (jarīsh) triggers liability according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Zufar, but not according to the two companions. The dispute hinges on whether the obligation is tied to the appearance of fruit (Abū Ḥanīfa), the ripeness leading to harvest (Abū Yūsuf), or actual harvesting and threshing (Muḥammad). The muftā bihī (relied-upon position) in the Ḥanafī school is often the view of Abū Yūsuf, as it is considered the most practical.

Key Terms
جرين (jarīn): The threshing floor where grain is gathered after harvest.
جريش (jarīsh): Raw, unripe grain that is not yet fully matured.
عشر (‘ushr): The tithe or one-tenth due on agricultural produce.
وسق (wasq): A measure of volume equal to 60 sa‘, approximately 212 kg, used for nisab of ‘ushr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a farmer eats raw grain (jarish) before harvest, he owes 'ushr according to:
A) Abu Hanifa and Zufar only.
B) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
C) All three imams.
D) Consensus.

Key Takeaway
The timing of 'ushr obligation varies among Hanafi imams; the practical consequence is early consumption liability under the earliest view, while the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's moderate position.

Nisab for Non-Wasq Crops: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the views of Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad on nisab for non-measurable produce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on nisab for non-wasq crops and apply the mufta bihi view to a new case.

Core Idea
For non-volume crops, nisab is by value (Abu Yusuf) or unit weight (Muhammad); value-based is relied upon.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about how to set the zakat threshold for produce that is not traditionally measured by volume (wasq), such as saffron and cotton. Both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree that 'ushr is wajib, but they differ on the method to determine the minimum amount (nisab). Understanding this dispute helps you apply zakat rules consistently across diverse agricultural products.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine what exactly the two imams disagree on: the method for establishing nisab for crops that cannot be measured by volume (kayl).
The issue is not whether zakat is due, but how much triggers it.
2
State Abu Yusuf's view and its basis
Abu Yusuf rules that the nisab is the value equivalent of 5 wasqs of the cheapest commodity that is measured by volume (e.g., corn). This uses value as a common denominator.
He relies on qiyas: just as measured crops are compared by volume, non-measured crops are compared by value.
3
State Muhammad's view and its basis
Muhammad rules that the nisab is five multiples of the highest weight unit by which the crop itself is measured. For cotton, he specifies 5 loads, each 300 ratls.
He uses direct analogy to the type: the unit appropriate for that crop (e.g., weight for cotton).
4
Compare the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is large: for a small but valuable crop like saffron, Abu Yusuf's value nisab is quickly reached, while Muhammad's weight nisab may be extremely high.
This affects whether a farmer must pay zakat in a given year.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and why
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Yusuf's value-based view because it is more practical and universally applicable, as noted by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah.
The tarjih authority: later Hanafi masters prefer practicality without rejecting Muhammad's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يوسف : فيما لا يوسق كالزعفران والقطن : يجـب فيـه العشر إذا بلغت قيمته قيمة خمسةِ أوسقٍ من أدنى ما يدخل تحت الوسق . وقال محمد : يجب العشر إذا بلغ الخارج خمسة أمثال من أعلـى مـا يُقدر به نوعه، فاعتبر في القطن : خمسة أحمال، كل حمل: ثلاثمائة من. * قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف فيما لا يوسق) : أي لا يكال، (كالزعفران والقطن: يجب فيه العشر إذا بلغت قيمته قيمة خمسة أوسق من أدنى ما يدخل تحت الوسق). قال صاحب «الهداية»(۱): كالذرة في زماننا. ونحن نقول: كالحمص والدخن في بلادنا(٢).
And Abū Yūsuf said: Concerning what is not measured by wasq, like saffron and cotton: it is obligatory to pay 'ushr when its value reaches the value of five wasqs of the cheapest that fits under wasq. And Muḥammad said: it is obligatory when the yield reaches five multiples of the highest unit by which its type is measured, and he considered in cotton: five loads, each load: three hundred [ratls?]. * His saying: (And Abū Yūsuf said concerning what is not measured by wasq) i.e., not measured by kail, (like saffron and cotton: it is obligatory to pay 'ushr when its value reaches the value of five wasqs of the cheapest that fits under wasq). The author of al-Hidayah said: like corn in our time. And we say: like chickpeas and millet in our country (Yemen).
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses the nisab for crops that are not measured by volume (wasq), such as saffron and cotton. Abū Yūsuf rules that the nisab is determined by value: the produce must be worth the value of five wasqs of the cheapest commodity that is measured by volume (e.g., corn). Muḥammad rules by weight: the nisab is five times the highest weight unit of that type (e.g., for cotton, five loads, each load 300 ratls). The commentator then explains the text and mentions that some later scholars (e.g., the author of Al-Hidayah) apply the value standard to common grains like corn, while the commentator himself notes that in Yemen they apply it to chickpeas and millet.

Key Terms
وسق (wasq): A measure of volume; here used as standard for nisab.
كال (kayl): Measuring by volume, as opposed to weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
عند أبي يوسف، زكاة الزعفران تجب إذا بلغت قيمته:
أ) قيمة خمسة أوسق من أدنى ما يكال.
ب) خمسة أحمال.
ج) قيمة خمسة أوسق من الزعفران.
د) قيمة خمسة أوسق من أعلاه.

Key Takeaway
The nisab for non-wasq crops is determined either by value equivalent of 5 wasqs (Abu Yusuf) or by unit weight (Muhammad); the value-based method is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Loss or Theft of Crops After Harvest: Effect on 'Ushr
States the rule regarding loss and theft after harvest and its effect on 'ushr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on loss or theft of harvested crops and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding counting the lost portion for nisab.

Core Idea
Loss of harvested crop does not exempt you from 'ushr; the lost portion counts towards nisab per the two companions.

You have harvested your crop, but before paying the 'ushr, part of it is stolen or destroyed. Do you still owe zakāt on it? Does the lost amount affect whether you have reached the minimum threshold? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position, including a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa's two main disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Focus on the text's scenario: harvested crop is lost or stolen after the obligation of 'ushr has become due (i.e., after harvest).
The text assumes the loss occurs after harvest but before payment.
2
Extract the Consensus
Note the agreed point: no 'ushr is due on the portion that is lost or stolen itself (by consensus).
This consensus is significant – the lost portion is not subject to zakāt directly.
3
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Spot the difference: The two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) count the lost portion toward completing the nisāb (5 wasqs), while Abū Yūsuf (in another narration) does not count it, requiring the nisāb to be present in the remaining produce alone.
This difference affects whether 'ushr is due on a smaller remaining amount.
4
Apply the Ruling on Compensation
Understand that if the owner later recovers compensation from the destroyer (mutlif), he must pay 'ushr on the compensated amount along with the 'ushr on what remained.
Compensation effectively restores the liability for the lost portion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تلف بغير صُنعه بعد حصاده، أو سُرق: فلا عُشر في الذاهب، بالإجماع، ويُحسب عليه في تمام الأوسق عندهما إن كان بعد الوجوب، حتى إن الباقي لو كان مع الذاهب خمسة أوسق: يجب العشر في الباقي، لا غير. وعن أبي يوسف : لا يُعتبر الذاهب ويُعتبر في الباقي خمسة أوسق، فإن أخذ من مُتلفه ضمانَه : أَدَّى عُشره وعشر ما بقي.
What perishes without his action after its harvest, or is stolen: there is no 'ushr on what is lost, by consensus, but it is counted toward completing the wasqs according to the two companions if after the obligation, such that if the remaining along with the lost is five wasqs, 'ushr is due on the remaining only, not otherwise. From Abū Yūsuf: the lost is not considered, and five wasqs are considered in the remaining. If he takes compensation from the destroyer, he pays its 'ushr and the 'ushr of what remained.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where harvested crop is lost or stolen before obligation is discharged. By consensus, no zakāt is due on the lost portion itself, but it is counted toward the nisāb threshold according to the two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) if the obligation had already become due. Abū Yūsuf is also reported to have a view that the lost portion is not counted, so nisāb must be present in the remaining alone. The text also rules that if the owner later recovers compensation from the one who destroyed or stole, he must pay 'ushr on the compensated amount along with whatever remained.

Key Terms
متلف (mutlif): The person who destroys or causes loss of the crop.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A farmer has 3 wasqs of grapes left after theft, with 3 wasqs stolen. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how much 'ushr is due?
A) 0.3 wasq
B) 0.6 wasq
C) 0 wasq
D) 0.6 wasq on the total

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, lost harvested crop still counts towards the nisab threshold, but 'ushr is only paid on what remains; if compensation is later received, 'ushr is also paid on that.

Liability for Eating from Jarin and Dispute on Lost Crop
States rulings on guarantee for eating from jarin and loss counting toward nisab with dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on eating from the jarin and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether lost crop counts toward the 'ushr nisab.

Core Idea
Eating from the jarin incurs liability; lost crop counts toward nisab according to the two, but not according to Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah presents two key rules from the Mukhtasar al-Quduri regarding crops after harvest. The first establishes a liability (daman) for eating from the threshing floor (jarin) by consensus. The second addresses what happens if the crop is destroyed or stolen after harvest: while there is consensus that no 'ushr is due on the lost portion, a dispute arises among the Hanafi imams on whether that lost portion counts toward completing the nisab for 'ushr on the remaining crop. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly calculating zakat on agricultural produce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage contains two distinct rulings: (1) eating from the jarin incurs compensation; (2) crop lost after harvest bears no 'ushr, but the lost amount may count toward nisab.
Both rulings are rooted in the fact that the 'ushr obligation attaches when the crop is in the jarin.
2
Note the consensus points
There is ijma' on (a) liability for eating from the jarin, and (b) no 'ushr on the lost portion itself.
The consensus is from the Hanafi school, but the text says 'ijma'an' implying within the school.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The dispute is whether the lost amount is counted toward the five-wasq nisab. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes; Abu Yusuf says no.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: if total was 5 wasqs and 1 wasq is lost, the two say 'ushr is due on 4 wasqs; Abu Yusuf says no 'ushr because remaining is only 4.
4
Understand the compensation scenario
If the destroyer (e.g., thief) later compensates the owner, the owner must pay 'ushr on that compensation along with 'ushr on the remaining crop, because the compensation substitutes for the lost crop.
This shows that the 'ushr obligation is not simply on physical crop but on the value received as substitute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أكل منها بعد ما صارت في الجرين: ضمن، إجماعاً. وما تلف بغير صُنعه بعد حصاده، أو سُرق: فلا عُشر في الذاهب، بالإجماع، ويُحسب عليه في تمام الأوسق عندهما إن كان بعد الوجوب، حتى إن الباقي لو كان مع الذاهب خمسة أوسق: يجب العشر في الباقي، لا غير. وعن أبي يوسف : لا يُعتبر الذاهب ويُعتبر في الباقي خمسة أوسق، فإن أخذ من مُتلفه ضمانَه : أَدَّى عُشره وعشر ما بقي.
And if he ate from it after it became in the jarin (threshing floor), he is liable (must compensate) by consensus. And whatever is destroyed without his action after its harvest, or stolen: there is no 'ushr on the lost (portion), by consensus, and it (the lost) is counted towards the completion of the wasqs according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) if it was after the obligation (became due); such that if the remaining together with the lost amounts to five wasqs: the 'ushr is obligatory on the remaining, not otherwise. And from Abu Yusuf: the lost is not considered, and only the remaining must reach five wasqs. And if he takes compensation from the one who destroyed it: he pays its 'ushr and the 'ushr of what remained.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents two related rulings from the Mukhtasar al-Quduri. First: if a person eats from his own crop after it has been placed in the threshing floor (jarin), he is liable to replace what he ate. This is by consensus (ijma'), because once the crop is gathered and the obligation of 'ushr has become due (by being in the jarin and ripe), the crop is a trust; consuming it without permission is a violation requiring compensation. Second: if the crop is destroyed without his act (e.g., natural disaster) or stolen, there is no 'ushr on the lost portion, by consensus. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises on whether the lost amount is counted toward the nisab. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), if the loss occurred after the 'ushr became wajib (i.e., after harvest and placement in jarin), the lost portion is counted to complete the five wasqs. So if the remaining plus the lost reaches five wasqs, 'ushr is due only on the remaining. According to Abu Yusuf, the lost is not counted; the remaining must itself be five wasqs for 'ushr to be due. Then, if the person who caused the destruction (mutlif) pays compensation, the owner must pay 'ushr on that compensation along with the 'ushr on the remaining crop, because the compensation substitutes for the lost crop.

Key Terms
الجرين (al-jarīn): The threshing floor where harvested crops are gathered and the 'ushr obligation becomes due.
الوسق (al-wasq): A measure of volume equal to 60 sa'; used to determine nisab for 'ushr on crops.
ضمن (ḍaman): Liability to compensate for damage or consumption of a trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a farmer's crop in the jarin is 5 wasqs, but 1 wasq is lost after harvest, how much 'ushr is due?
A) 'Ushr on the remaining 4 wasqs.
B) No 'ushr because the remaining is only 4 wasqs.
C) 'Ushr on the lost portion only.
D) 'Ushr on the full 5 wasqs.

Key Takeaway
Eating from the jarin incurs liability; lost crop is counted toward nisab per the majority Hanafi view, but Abu Yusuf disagrees.

Nisab for Items Not Measured by Volume: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
Presents differing views on nisab for saffron, cotton, and similar items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi opinions on the nisab for non-volume crops and apply the mufta-bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
For crops not measured by volume, nisab for 'ushr is either value-based (Abu Yusuf) or measure-based (Muhammad) — the latter is preferred.

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, with commentary from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on how to determine the nisab for crops like saffron and cotton, which are sold by weight rather than volume. Both agree that 'ushr is obligatory, but they differ on the threshold. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly applying zakat to non-staple cash crops.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common ground
Note that both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree that 'ushr (10% zakat) is wajib on crops like saffron and cotton. The dispute is solely about the nisab threshold.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf ties the nisab to the market value of the crop. He requires that the crop's value equal the value of five wasqs of the cheapest grain that is measured by volume (e.g., barley, millet). This means the nisab fluctuates with grain prices.
The cheapest grain is used to avoid hardship.
3
Analyze Muhammad's position
Muhammad uses a fixed measure based on the crop's own type. For cotton: 5 loads (ahmal), each 300 mann. For saffron: 5 mann (1 mann = 26 ounces). The nisab is thus stable and independent of other commodity prices.
These measures are customary for each crop.
4
Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Apply to a scenario: A farmer harvests 4 loads of cotton worth the price of 5 wasqs of barley. Under Abu Yusuf, 'ushr is due; under Muhammad, it is not. The mufta bihi (preferred) view is Muhammad's, so 'ushr would not be due.
Later Hanafi practice favors Muhammad's view for ease of calculation.
5
Recognize the commentary expansion
The commentator clarifies terms: 'what is not measured by wasq' means not measured by volume (kayl). He gives examples of cheap grains for Abu Yusuf's benchmark (millet, chickpeas in Yemen). He also defines the mann precisely.
This shows how classical texts are contextualized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يوسف : فيما لا يوسق كالزعفران والقطن : يَجِبُ فيه العُشر إذا بلغت قيمته قيمة خمسةِ أوسقٍ من أدنى ما يدخل تحت الوسق . وقال محمد : يَجِبُ العُشر إذا بلغ الخارج خمسة أمثال من أعلَى ما يُقدر به نوعه، فاعتبر في القطن : خمسة أحمال، وفي الزعفران : خمسة أمناء . * قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف فيما لا يوسق) : أي لا يكال، (كالزعفران والقطن: يجب فيه العشر إذا بلغت قيمته قيمة خمسة أوسق من أدنى ما يدخل تحت الوسق). قال صاحب «الهداية»: كالذرة في زماننا. ونحن نقول: كالحمص والدخن في بلادنا. * قوله: (وقال محمد: يجب العشر إذا بلغ الخارج خمسة أمثال أعلى ما يُقدر به نوعه، فاعتبر في القُطن خمسة أحمال)، كل حمل: ثلاثمائة من . وفي الزعفران : خمسة أمناء . (وفي الزعفران خمسة أمنان) ، والمَنُ: ستة وعشرون أوقية، والأوقية: سبعة مثاقيل، وهي عشرة دراهم.
And Abu Yusuf said: regarding what is not measured by wasq (like saffron and cotton): 'ushr is due upon it if its value reaches the value of five wasqs of the cheapest item that falls under wasq. And Muhammad said: 'ushr is due if the yield reaches five times the highest measure of its type, so he considered for cotton: five loads (ahmal), and for saffron: five mann. Commentary: His statement: 'And Abu Yusuf said regarding what is not measured by wasq' i.e., what is not measured by volume (yukal) 'like saffron and cotton: 'ushr is due if its value reaches the value of five wasqs of the cheapest item that falls under wasq.' The author of al-Hidayah said: like millet (dhurrah) in our time. And we say: like chickpeas (himmas) and millet (dukhn) in our lands. His statement: 'And Muhammad said: 'ushr is due if the yield reaches five times the highest measure of its type, so he considered for cotton five loads (ahmal), each load: three hundred mann. And for saffron: five mann.' (For saffron five mann) and the mann: twenty-six ounces, and the ounce: seven mithqals, which is ten dirhams.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the nisab for crops that are not measured by volume (yukal), such as saffron and cotton. The matn from al-Quduri first states both positions. Abu Yusuf holds that 'ushr becomes obligatory on such items when their value reaches the value of five wasqs of the cheapest item that is measured by wasq (e.g., cheap cereals like barley). The commentator explains that 'what is not measured by wasq' means what is not measured by volume (kayl). The author of al-Hidayah gives an example: like millet (dhurrah) in their time as the cheapest wasq-item. The commentator adds that in their own land (Yemen), chickpeas and millet (dukhn) serve as the benchmark. Muhammad holds that 'ushr is due when the produce itself reaches five times the highest measure of its own type. For cotton, that is five loads (ahmal), each load being three hundred mann. For saffron, five mann. The commentary then defines the mann as twenty-six ounces, each ounce being seven mithqals (weight) or ten dirhams (silver coins). The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf's view, the nisab depends on the fluctuating market value of the cheapest grain; under Muhammad's view, it is a fixed weight/measure of the specific crop. Later Hanafi practice often follows Muhammad's view for consistency.

Key Terms
لا يوسق (lā yūsaq): Items that are not measured by volume (wasq); typically sold by weight, like cotton and saffron.
الحمل (al-ḥiml): A measure of weight for cotton, specified as 300 mann per load.
المَنّ (al-mann): A unit of weight; 1 mann = 26 ounces = 182 dirhams (approximately 580 grams).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, what is the nisab for cotton for 'ushr?
A) Value equal to 5 wasqs of the cheapest grain.
B) Five loads (ahmal), each = 300 mann.
C) Five wasqs in volume.
D) Five mann.

Key Takeaway
For non-volume crops, the preferred Hanafi nisab for 'ushr is a fixed measure of the crop itself, not its market value.
